Allah's Existence
beginner
6 min
Allah is the Noblest of All Existents: Proofs from Self-Sufficiency and Need
Al-Razi provides three logical arguments for Allah being the noblest existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the concepts of self-sufficiency, necessity, and perpetual dependence to prove that Allah is the noblest of all existents.

Core Idea
Allah's absolute independence and everything else's total dependence prove His supreme nobility.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a multi-faceted proof that Allah is the most noble (ashraf) of all existents. The argument builds on two key distinctions: between the self-sufficient and the needy, and between the Necessary and the possible. By examining these relationships, al-Razi shows that nobility belongs only to the One who is independent of all, while everything else is dependent on Him in every respect, including their continued existence and even their non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting that al-Razi presents three distinct lines of reasoning. Try to identify each separate argument as you read.
The arguments are introduced by 'منا' (including) and 'ايضا' (also).
2
Understand the first argument: self-sufficiency and simplicity
The first argument (lines 1-2) relies on Allah's being ghani (free of need) from any agent or goal, and being fard (absolute individual) without parts. Contrast this with everything else's need and compositeness.
3
Understand the second argument: necessity vs. possibility
The second argument (lines 2-4) uses the classification of existents into the Necessary Beings (wajib li-dhatihi) and possible beings (mumkin li-dhatihi). Since only Allah is necessary and everything else is possible and needs an effecter, He is the sole source of all, making Him most noble.
4
Understand the third argument: perpetual need including non-existence
The third argument (lines 4-8) extends the need of the possible to both continuance (baqa') and non-existence. The possible is always dependent on a cause for existence and for non-existence; thus every moment of every thing is tied to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل عليه وجوه منها انه غني عن الفاعل والغاية وغيره محتاج اليه وانه فرد على الاطلاق فهو غني عن الجزء المقوم. ان الواجب لذاته ليس الا هو وكل ما سواه هو ممكن لذاته محتاج الى المؤثر فيلزم ان كل ما سواه هو محتاج اليه وهو غني عن كل ما سواه فوجب ان يكون اشرف الموجودات. وايضا ثبت ان الممكن كما انه محتاج الى المؤثر حال حدوثه فهو محتاج اليه ايضا حال بقائه وكل ما سواه فهو محتاج اليه في جميع اوقاته سواء كان ذلك حال الحدوث او حال البقاء وكما انه محتاج اليه في جانب الوجود ففي جانب العدم ايضا كذلك لما ثبت ان الممكن ليس معدوم الذات بل علة عدمه عدم العلة فثبت بما ذكرنا ان الحق سبحانه وتعالى اشرف من غيره بحسب هذه الاعتبارات.
And it is indicated by aspects, including that He is free of need of agent and goal, and other than Him is in need of Him, and that He is an absolute individual, so He is free of need of a constituent part. That the Necessary Existent is none but Him, and everything other than Him is possible in itself, in need of an effecter. So it follows that everything other than Him is in need of Him, and He is free of need of everything other than Him, so He must be the noblest of existents. And also it is established that the possible, just as it is in need of an effecter at the time of its origination, is also in need of Him at the time of its continuance, and everything other than Him is in need of Him at all its times, whether that be at origination or continuance, and just as it is in need of Him with respect to existence, so also with respect to non-existence, because it is established that the possible is not non-existent in essence, but rather the cause of its non-existence is the non-existence of the cause. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the Truth, glorified and exalted, is nobler than other than Him according to these considerations.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage provides the proof (the 'aspects' mentioned) that Allah is the most noble of existents. The first argument: Allah is self-sufficient (ghani) from any external agent or purpose, while everything else is in need of Him. He is an absolute individual (fard), not composed of parts, so He does not need a constituent part (juz' muqawwim). The second argument: The Necessary Existent (wajib li-dhatihi) is only Allah; everything else is possible (mumkin) in itself and therefore requires an effecter (mu'aththir) for its existence. Hence, everything else is dependent on Him, and He is independent of them, making Him the noblest. The third argument: The possible is in need of the effecter not only at the moment of origination (huduth) but also for its continued existence (baqa'), so everything other than Allah is perpetually in need of Him. Additionally, this need extends to non-existence: the possible does not become non-existent by itself; its non-existence is due to the absence of the cause. Thus, in all respects, Allah is the most noble.

فاعل

ممكن لذاته
مؤثر

علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that Allah's self-sufficiency means He has no relation to creation.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that all need is directed to Him; His self-sufficiency does not mean isolation, but rather that He is the source of all dependence.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why is everything other than Allah in need of Him at all times?
A) Because they were created and then become independent
B) Because they are possible in themselves and need an effecter for both origination and continuance
C) Because they are composed of parts and need a unifier
D) Because they are non-existent and need a creator

Key Takeaway
Through three logical arguments—self-sufficiency, necessity vs. possibility, and perpetual dependence including non-existence—al-Razi demonstrates that Allah is the noblest of all existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Everything other than Allah is in need of Him at every moment of existence and non-existence. Look at your daily life—where do you unconsciously think you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly acknowledged that you need Allah for your very next heartbeat?
?
Al-Razi proves that Allah is the noblest precisely because He needs nothing and everything needs Him. How does this truth confront your pride? Where have you been seeking nobility apart from Him?
?
The passage shows that even your non-existence depends on Allah—you are not even the master of your own death. If you believe this, how should it reshape your trust in Allah's plan for your life and your ultimate return to Him? What is one concrete action you can take today to live in this awareness?

Proof of the Pre-Eternal Creator from Temporal Events
Proof that temporal events necessitate a pre-eternal creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that temporal events necessitate a pre-eternal creator, and why the chain of causes cannot regress infinitely.

Core Idea
Every temporal event points to a pre-eternal cause that is necessary.

This passage presents a foundational proof in Islamic theology: the world is filled with temporal events, each requiring a cause. If we trace these causes back, we cannot go on infinitely—there must be a first cause that is pre-eternal and necessary. This cause is God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage (even with OCR issues) and the English translation. Notice the argument's flow: temporal events require a cause; infinite regress is invalid; thus a pre-eternal necessary being exists.
Focus on the logical structure rather than the corrupted words.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the terms: temporal event (حادث), possible being (ممكن), cause (موثر), pre-eternity (قدم). Understand how they relate: a temporal event is contingent and needs a cause; a possible being depends on a necessary being.
3
Trace the argument's logic
Follow the argument: The existence of temporal events implies a chain of causes. If the chain were infinite, there would be no ultimate cause, which is impossible. Therefore, there must be a first cause that is pre-eternal and uncaused.
This is the classic kalam cosmological argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the argument does not claim the universe had a beginning in time, but that every temporal event requires a cause outside itself. The first cause is necessary and pre-eternal.
This proof establishes God's existence without assuming a temporal beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن هذا العلم [؟] منواله الله [؟] تزره [؟] وليس على كنه و با شتر [؟] وتقرير هذا المطلوب أن يقول [؟] إذا كان هو التيار تكون نسبة الجبر و اليد كنبتة القدم اليه وما دام موهرها الاسترا روانه [؟] وهذا يدل على أن صلح الحاجة مالا مكان [؟] ثم قال [؟] إن تحمل هذا الرجمان هويات الات الضرورتها موجوده لزم كون هذا الرجمات من حيث ان المحل سابق عل المال وذات دورة الاور باطل [؟] و نسبت ان الممكن لا يكن دخوله في الوجبر والا لاجل موثر [؟] هذا التجوال في موضع من المواضع على حصول الموفر و الجمع وهذا العالى باطل فذلك المقدم امضاء ما مات اللازمة فهو بات [؟] لا ذلك الرجال عميا عن الموفر اشوق افتقار المالموثر في شي من المواقع [؟] لا ذاكان و جهان اساطير والمكن علاة رغنيا عن الموتر من حيثه هولاء هذا الاستغنا ما سكول [؟] وانا بيان ان وجمان احد طرق ممكن تلو الآخر تو ترند العمال مصر والموفر قلانه العلم اليه [؟]
Know that this science [؟] ... and the clarification of this desired point is to say [؟] if it is the [؟] ... and this indicates that [؟] ... then he said [؟] that the occurrence of this description is necessary existences, it follows that this description [؟] ... and it is related that the possible does not enter into necessity except due to an effect [؟] ... this is invalid because the premise [؟] ... this need for the effect in any of the cases [؟] ... it is not so, for if the possible is independent of the effect [؟] ... this independence is not correct [؟] and I explain that one of the ways of the possible after another [؟] ...

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a pre-eternal creator based on the nature of temporal events. The argument begins by stating that every temporal event requires a cause, and if the chain of causes goes back infinitely, it would lead to a vicious regress. Therefore, there must be a first cause that is pre-eternal and necessary. The text then discusses the relationship between the possible and the necessary, emphasizing that the possible depends on the necessary for its existence. The proof concludes that the existence of temporal events necessitates a pre-eternal cause, which is God.

Key Takeaway
The existence of temporal events logically requires a pre-eternal, necessary cause—God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if every event in your life has a purpose from the pre-eternal Creator, or do you attribute your successes and failures to random chance? In what area today can you consciously recognize His causal role?
?
If you truly believed that your very existence is contingent on the Necessary Being every moment, how would it change the way you pray or rely on Him? What would you do differently tonight?
?
In what specific area of your life are you acting as if you are self-sufficient (like a possible being claiming independence), and how can you affirm your dependence on God in that area starting now?

Defining Iḍlāl as Creating Disbelief and Misguidance
Sanusi defines iḍlāl and gives a Qur’anic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'iḍlāl' (leading astray) is defined as God's creation of disbelief and misguidance, not as mere abandonment or coercion.

Core Idea
Iḍlāl is creation of disbelief, not coercion.

The author begins by establishing the precise meaning of 'iḍlāl'. He asserts that it is an expression for creating disbelief (kufr) and misguidance (ḍalāl) in the hearts of certain individuals. To support this, he cites a Qur'anic verse about sending messengers in the language of their people, showing that God's guidance is complete and that misguidance is also His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of iḍlāl
Carefully read the opening statement: 'iḍlāl is an expression for creating disbelief and misguidance.' Notice the direct link between iḍlāl and creation (khalq).
The author uses the term 'ibāra 'an' to indicate a definition.
2
Understand the Qur’anic proof
Examine the verse Q 14:4 (about messengers speaking the language of their people). The author argues that the clarification is sufficient; guidance is complete. This implies that any remaining misguidance is also from God.
The verse is used to show that God provides clarity, and if some still go astray, it is by His creation.
3
Note the author’s conclusion on misguidance
The passage concludes: 'al-ḍalāl huwa khalq al-ḍalāl' (misguidance is creating misguidance). This reinforces that iḍlāl is not merely leaving someone alone but actively creating the state of disbelief.
This is a key affirmation of divine causality in disbelief.

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'iḍlāl' (leading astray) as God's creation of disbelief and misguidance in the hearts of certain individuals, using the examples of Pharaoh, Iblis, and Samiri (implied from the garbled text). The author begins by stating that 'iḍlāl' is an expression for creating kufr and ḍalāl. He then quotes a Qur'anic verse (Q 14:4) about sending messengers in the language of their people, arguing that the clarification is sufficient and that God's guidance is complete. The author asserts that misguidance (al-ḍalāl) itself is created by God, as stated in the conclusion: 'misguidance is creating misguidance' (al-ḍalāl huwa khalq al-ḍalāl).

On page 28, the author refutes six objections (al-wujūh) against the doctrine that God leads astray. Each objection is briefly presented and then dismissed. The first objection concerns the possibility of God's power and the necessity of His action; the second relates to obligation (taklīf) and God's imposition of duties; the third involves the meaning of divine intent; the fourth addresses God's foreknowledge of disbelief; the fifth concerns the interpretation of a specific verse; and the sixth deals with supplication for protection from Satan. The author concludes that all these objections collapse ('saqūṭ jami' hādhihi al-wujūh'), affirming that the text (al-naṣṣ al-ṣarīḥ) clearly supports the position that God creates both guidance and misguidance as He wills.

Key Terms
إضلال
ضلال
خلق الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iḍlāl means God forces disbelief against one's will or merely abandons the person.
Correction: The text shows iḍlāl is the creation of disbelief itself, a positive act of making a person disbelieve, not coercion or neglect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'iḍlāl' mean?
A) Withholding guidance and letting people choose
B) Creating disbelief and misguidance
C) Forcing someone to disbelieve against their will
D) Punishing disbelievers in this world

Key Takeaway
Iḍlāl is God's creation of disbelief and misguidance, not merely abandonment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly acknowledge that your own īmān (faith) is a gift of hidāya from Allah, not earned by your deeds? Take a moment to thank Him specifically for not creating kufr in your heart. What would you be without His guidance?
?
Think of someone whose disbelief troubles you. According to this lesson, their disbelief is created by Allah. How does this affect your attitude toward them? Does it lead you to humbly pray for them, or to pride? Repent if needed and commit to making du'a for them today.
?
If you ever feel doubts creeping into your heart, what does this lesson teach you to do? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to seek guidance from Allah, knowing that iḍlāl is not mere abandonment but a created state that only He can remove.

Refutation of Objections against Divine Leading Astray
Sanusi refutes six objections against iḍlāl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six objections raised against the doctrine that God creates disbelief, and why the author declares them all invalid.

Core Idea
All objections to divine iḍlāl collapse under examination.

After defining iḍlāl as creation of disbelief, the author anticipates and refutes six objections. These objections challenge the notion that God leads people astray, often appealing to divine justice, power, or wisdom. The author systematically dismisses each, concluding that the clear scriptural evidence (al-naṣṣ al-ṣarīḥ) stands firm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six objections
Read the section listing the objections (from 'قال ابو مسلم' onward). Recognize markers like 'والأول' (the first), 'والثاني' (the second), etc.
The objections are numbered but the OCR may obscure them. Look for numeric indicators.
2
Understand the author's refutation for each
For each objection, note the author's counter-argument. He often points to logical flaws or misapplications of divine attributes.
The refutations are brief; focus on the key point of collapse.
3
Appreciate the final conclusion
The author ends by stating all aspects (jami' hādhihi al-wujūh) are fallen (sāqiṭa), affirming the explicit text (al-naṣṣ al-ṣarīḥ).
This shows the author's confidence in the scriptural proof.

Source Text

Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'iḍlāl' (leading astray) as God's creation of disbelief and misguidance in the hearts of certain individuals, using the examples of Pharaoh, Iblis, and Samiri (implied from the garbled text). The author begins by stating that 'iḍlāl' is an expression for creating kufr and ḍalāl. He then quotes a Qur'anic verse (Q 14:4) about sending messengers in the language of their people, arguing that the clarification is sufficient and that God's guidance is complete. The author asserts that misguidance (al-ḍalāl) itself is created by God, as stated in the conclusion: 'misguidance is creating misguidance' (al-ḍalāl huwa khalq al-ḍalāl).

On page 28, the author refutes six objections (al-wujūh) against the doctrine that God leads astray. Each objection is briefly presented and then dismissed. The first objection concerns the possibility of God's power and the necessity of His action; the second relates to obligation (taklīf) and God's imposition of duties; the third involves the meaning of divine intent; the fourth addresses God's foreknowledge of disbelief; the fifth concerns the interpretation of a specific verse; and the sixth deals with supplication for protection from Satan. The author concludes that all these objections collapse ('saqūṭ jami' hādhihi al-wujūh'), affirming that the text (al-naṣṣ al-ṣarīḥ) clearly supports the position that God creates both guidance and misguidance as He wills.

Key Terms
اعتراضات
أبو مسلم الأصفهاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates disbelief, it would be unjust or incompatible with human responsibility.
Correction: The author shows that objections fail and that divine iḍlāl is consistent with God's wisdom and power.

Key Takeaway
All six objections against divine iḍlāl are invalid; the scriptural proof stands.

?

The Four Causal Categories of Existents
Sanusi classifies existents into four causal categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four rational categories of existents based on causation and why this classification is foundational for proving God's uniqueness.

Core Idea
All existents are either causes, effects, both, or neither — only God is the pure cause.

Before we can discuss God's relationship to creation, we need a clear framework for thinking about causality. The author begins by dividing all that exists into four mutually exclusive categories based on whether something causes, is caused, both, or neither. This set-up leads directly to identifying which category belongs to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the four categories presented in a single sentence.
The Arabic uses 'إما أن' to enumerate the four possibilities.
2
Identify each category
List the four categories: (1) causes but is not affected, (2) receives effect but may not cause, (3) both causes and is affected, (4) neither causes nor is affected.
The second category is partially uncertain in the text but the idea is clear.
3
Reflect on the fourth category
Consider whether any contingent being can truly be neither cause nor effect. The author will later show that only the Necessary Being fits the first category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الموجودات بالنسية العقلية على أربعة أقسام : لأنها إما أن يكون مؤثراً ولا يتأثر بوجه من الوجوه ، وإما أن يقبل الأثر [؟] بعد أن [؟] ، وإما أن يؤثر ويتأثر معاً ، وإما أن لا يؤثر ولا يتأثر البتة . نهر [؟] الله سبحانه وتعالى والدليل عليه أنه واجب الوجود لذاته وكل ما هو واجب الوجود لذاته فإنه واجب الوجود في جميع صفاته السلبية والثبوتية . والدليل عليه : أن ذاته إن كانت كافية في حصول ذلك الإيجاب وذلك السلب لزم أن يدوم ذلك الإيجاب وذلك السلب بدوام ذاته ، [؟] وإن لم تكن ذاته كافية في حصول ذلك الإيجاب وذلك السلب كان موقوفاً على حصول ذلك الإيجاب وذلك السلب والموقوف على الغير موقوف على الغير والموقوف على الغير ليس بواجب الوجود لذاته . فثبت أن الواجب الوجود لذاته لا يتأثر ولا يقبل الأثر من غيره . وثبت أيضاً أنه لا يؤثر فيه غيره لأنه لو أثر فيه غيره لكان متأثراً وقد ثبت أنه لا يتأثر . فثبت أن الله سبحانه وتعالى هو المؤثر الذي لا يتأثر بوجه من الوجوه.
Know that existents in relation to the intellect are of four divisions: because they are either an effecter and are not affected in any way, or they receive effect [؟] after [؟], or they effect and are affected together, or they neither effect nor are affected at all. A river [؟] God, glorified and exalted, and the proof for it is that He is necessary of existence by essence, and everything that is necessary of existence by essence is necessary of existence in all His attributes, negative and positive. And the proof for that: that His essence, if it were sufficient for the attainment of that affirmation and that negation, it would necessitate that that affirmation and that negation endure with the endurance of His essence, [؟]; and if His essence were not sufficient for the attainment of that affirmation and that negation, it would be dependent on the attainment of that affirmation and that negation, and what is dependent on another is dependent on another, and what is dependent on another is not necessary of existence by essence. So it is established that the necessary of existence by essence is not affected and does not receive effect from another. And it is also established that nothing else effects Him, because if something else effected Him, He would be affected, and it has been established that He is not affected. So it is established that God, glorified and exalted, is the effecter who is not affected in any way.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of existents and a proof of God's uniqueness. The author first divides all existents rationally into four categories based on causality: (1) something that causes but is never caused; (2) something that receives an effect after being caused (i.e., is caused but does not cause); (3) something that both causes and is caused; (4) something that neither causes nor is caused. This classification sets the stage to identify which category applies to God. The author then argues that God, being necessary of existence by essence, must belong to the first category: He is the effecter who is never affected. The proof proceeds by showing that whatever is necessary in essence must also be necessary in all its attributes—both negative (e.g., not being affected) and positive. If His essence were not sufficient for a given attribute, that attribute would depend on something else, contradicting His necessary existence. Therefore, God cannot be affected by anything, nor can anything affect Him. Thus He is the sole uncaused cause.

Key Terms
موجود
مؤثر
متأثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many categories does the author present for existents?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 4
D) 5

Key Takeaway
The four-category classification provides the logical framework for identifying God as the unique uncaused cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you identify one person or thing in your life that you treat as if it causes effects without ever being affected—something you rely on absolutely, like your job, health, or a relationship? How does this classification remind you that only God is the true uncaused cause? This week, try to consciously attribute every good outcome first to Him.
?
When you receive a blessing today, do you immediately think of the human or material cause (your effort, the person who gave it) without acknowledging the ultimate Cause? What would change in your heart if you traced every single cause back to God? Commit to saying 'Allah is the Causer' before thanking the immediate cause.
?
The fourth category of existents (neither causes nor is affected) is mysterious—it seems to describe something completely independent. Do you have any area of your life where you pretend you are independent, needing no one and affecting no one? How does this category expose the lie of self-sufficiency, and what step can you take today to admit your total dependence on God?

Proof That the Necessary Being Is Not Affected
Sanusi proves that the Necessary Being cannot be affected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being must be absolutely unaffected and how this proof establishes God's independence.

Core Idea
Whatever is necessary in essence cannot depend on anything else for any attribute.

Having classified existents, the author now argues that the Necessary Being belongs to the first category: the pure cause. The proof hinges on the fact that a being necessary in essence cannot have any attribute that depends on another. This leads to the conclusion that God is the effecter who is not affected.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the conditional reasoning about the essence's sufficiency.
The text has uncertain words marked by [؟], but the logical structure is clear.
2
Follow the conditional argument
The author considers two cases: if the essence is sufficient for an attribute, it endures with the essence; if not sufficient, the attribute depends on something else, contradicting necessary existence.
This shows that all attributes of the Necessary Being are necessary and intrinsic.
3
Understand the final conclusion
From the argument, it follows that the Necessary Being cannot be affected by anything, nor can anything affect Him. Thus He is the effecter who is not affected.
This directly places God in the first category of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot be affected by your sins or your obedience, then your actions only harm or benefit yourself. Knowing this, how does it change the way you approach disobedience? Will you continue to sin knowing that only you are harmed?
?
The Necessary Being depends on nothing. Examine your life: in what areas are you still relying on created things (people, wealth, status) rather than depending solely on Allah? What would it practically mean for you to shift that dependence to Him alone?
?
Since God is the effecter who is not affected, He is not changed by your worship. Your worship does not 'move' Him; it is purely for your own good. Does your worship arise from a desire to 'affect' God (to gain His favor or avoid His wrath) or from recognition of His absolute perfection? How can you purify your intention to be solely for His sake?

The Fifth Proof: Existence Nobler Than Non-Existence
Author presents the fifth proof that God is noblest because existence is nobler than non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth rational proof that God is the noblest of existents because existence is nobler than non-existence and God alone is existent by essence.

Core Idea
Existence is nobler than non-existence, so the necessarily existent God is the noblest being.

This lesson presents the fifth of the author's proofs for the nobility of God. The argument begins with a basic value judgment: existence is superior to non-existence. From this, the author shows that the Necessary Being, who cannot not exist, is therefore the noblest. The passage also distinguishes between God as Truth (al-Haqq) and all else as falsehood, grounding this in the nature of existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The argument starts with the claim that existence is nobler than non-existence. This is a fundamental value judgment accepted in philosophical theology.
Reflect on why existence might be considered inherently better than non-existence.
2
Apply to the Necessary Being
The Necessary Being (wajib li-dhatihi) does not accept non-existence at all; He is existent by His own essence and His existence is the opposite of non-existence.
Notice how the author emphasizes that His existence is 'as if contrary to non-existence'.
3
Contrast with contingent beings
Contingent beings (mumkin li-dhatihi) are non-existent when considered in themselves; they only exist through another.
This contrast highlights the unique status of the Necessary Being.
4
Conclude God as Truth, others falsehood
Because God alone is existent by essence, He is called Truth (Haqq), while everything else is falsehood (batil). The Quranic verse confirms this.
The term 'Truth' here denotes permanent, self-subsistent reality.
5
Recognize the final conclusion
Therefore, God is the noblest and most perfect of existents, and knowledge of Him is the noblest knowledge.
The argument ends by elevating both God and the knowledge of Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس: هو أن الوجود أشرف من العدم، والواجب لذاته لا يقبل العدم البتة فهو موجود لذاته، وبوجوده يحصل الموجود لكل موجود، بل وجوده كالمنافي للعدم وأما كل ما سواه فإنه ممكن لذاته، والممكن لذاته إذا نظر إليه من حيث هو هو وجد غير موجود وكل ما سواه فإنه إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو، لم يكن موجوداً. وهو سبحانه إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو، فهو الموجود. فلهذا المعنى قلنا: إنه حق وما سواه باطل بل الحق أنه لا يليق لفظ الحق إلا له ولا يليق وصف الاعتقاد بأنه حق، إلا باعتقاد وجوده. وأن كل ما سواء فهو الفناء المحض، والهلاك المحض، كما قال في الكتاب الإلهي: ﴿كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه﴾ فثبت بهذه الاعتبارات أنه تعالى أشرف الموجودات وأكملها، بل إنه تعالى أشرف وأكمل من أن يقاس هو إلى غيره، فإنه أشرف وأكمل منه. فكلما كان المعلوم أشرف، كان العلم به أشرف [ولما كان أن أشرف] المعلومات.
And the fifth: that existence is nobler than non-existence, and the Necessary in Its essence does not accept non-existence at all, so It is existent in Its essence, and by Its existence the existent is obtained for every existent, rather Its existence is as if contrary to non-existence. As for everything other than It, it is possible in its essence, and the possible in its essence, when considered in itself, is found to be non-existent. And everything other than It, if considered in itself, is not existent. He, glorified, if considered in Himself, is existent. For this meaning we say: He is the Truth and everything else is falsehood. Rather the truth is that the term 'Truth' is not fitting except for Him, nor is the description of belief as truth fitting except by believing in His existence. And that everything other than Him is pure annihilation and pure destruction, as He said in the divine book: 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' So it is established by these considerations that He, exalted, is the noblest of existents and the most perfect, rather He, exalted, is too noble and perfect to be compared to others, for He is nobler and more perfect than them. So whenever the known is nobler, knowledge of it is nobler, and since He is the noblest of known things.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the fifth argument) for the superiority of God over all else. The author begins by establishing a premise: existence (wujud) is nobler than non-existence (adam). The Necessary Being (al-wajib li-dhatihi) does not accept non-existence at all; He is existent by essence. Moreover, His existence is the source for every other existent. In contrast, everything contingent (al-mumkin) is not existent when considered in itself; it only exists through another. Hence, God is called 'Truth' (Haqq) because He is existent in Himself, while everything else is 'falsehood' (batil) because it is non-existent in itself. The author quotes the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' to reinforce that only God is truly existent and eternal. From this, he concludes that God is the noblest and most perfect of existents, so knowledge of Him is the noblest knowledge.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
عدم (adam)
واجب لذاته (wajib li-dhatihi)
ممكن لذاته (mumkin li-dhatihi)
حق (haqq)
باطل (batil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's nobility is relative to creation—that He is 'better' than others in a comparative sense.
Correction: The proof shows that God's nobility is absolute and intrinsic, based on the superiority of existence over non-existence and His own necessary existence. Others are not even existent in themselves, so the comparison is not relative but categorical.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, why is God alone deserving of the name 'Truth' (Haqq)?
A) Because He is necessary in essence while everything else is possible.
B) Because He is existent in Himself while everything else is non-existent in itself.
C) Because He is the creator of all things.
D) Because He is eternal and unchanging.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof demonstrates that God is the noblest being because existence itself is nobler than non-existence, and God alone is existent by His own essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you ever feel as if you are the source of your own existence? How does this lesson's claim that you are 'non-existent in yourself' challenge that feeling, and what would it mean to truly live knowing that only God is the Truth?
?
If everything other than God is 'pure annihilation and destruction,' what are you holding onto that you must let go of? Name one attachment you have that you treat as if it will last forever, and commit to one action today to put God's existence above it.
?
The lesson says that knowledge of the noblest known thing is the noblest knowledge. What is your current attitude toward learning about God? If you truly believed that studying Allah is the highest pursuit, how would you change your daily priorities starting tomorrow?

Knowledge of God: The Head of Spiritual Happiness
The author identifies knowledge of the First Principle as the head of spiritual happiness and argues for the superiority of this science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of God is considered the head of spiritual happiness and the foundation of all sciences.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God is the ultimate happiness and the head of all sciences.

In this passage, the author establishes the supreme value of the science of kalam by grounding it in the highest human aim: spiritual happiness. He argues that ignorance of God leads to eternal loss, while knowledge leads to eternal bliss. This sets the stage for the entire discipline.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author builds his argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two sources of evidence
The author mentions 'prophetic testimonies' and 'philosophical landmarks' as proofs. Think about what these refer to.
Prophetic = revelation, philosophical = rational proofs.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that this science is the head of all sciences. Consider why that follows from the premises.

ولا شك أن رأس السعادات الروحانية ورئيسهــا و(زبدتها) وخلاصتها : معرفة المبدأ الأول ومعرفة صفات جلاله ونعوت كماله وكبريائه وأيضا قد دلت الشواهد النبوية والمعالم الحكمية على أن الجهل بهذا الباب يوجب العذاب الدائم والخسار المطلق، وأن الفوز بهذه المعرفة يوجب السعادة الأبدية والسيادة السرمدية. فوجب أن يكون هذا العلم رأس جميع العلوم ورئيسها، وأشرف أقسامها وأجلها.
And there is no doubt that the head of spiritual happinesses and their leader and (their cream) and their essence is knowledge of the First Principle and knowledge of the attributes of His majesty and the qualities of His perfection and His greatness. And also, prophetic testimonies and philosophical landmarks have indicated that ignorance of this matter necessitates eternal torment and absolute loss, and that attainment of this knowledge necessitates eternal happiness and everlasting sovereignty. So it is necessary that this science be the head of all sciences and their leader, and the noblest of their divisions and the greatest of them.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage establishes the second proof for the honor of this science (ilm al-kalam). The author asserts without doubt that the foremost spiritual happiness is knowledge of the First Principle (God) and His attributes of majesty, perfection, and greatness. Both prophetic testimonies (revealed texts) and philosophical landmarks (rational proofs) indicate that ignorance of this subject leads to eternal punishment and total loss, while attaining this knowledge leads to eternal happiness and everlasting sovereignty. Therefore, this science—which treats knowledge of God—must be the head of all sciences, their leader, the noblest and greatest of their divisions.

Misconception: Some may think spiritual happiness is found in bodily pleasures or worldly achievements.
Correction: The author shows that true spiritual happiness is knowledge of God, and ignorance leads to eternal loss.

Knowledge of God is the supreme spiritual happiness and the foundation of all sciences.

Applying the Self-Knowledge Paradox to Knowledge of God
Sanusi applies the self-knowledge paradox to argue for the necessity of analogical knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the paradox of self-knowledge (being both the clearest and most difficult) is used to argue that human intellect cannot fully comprehend God and must rely on analogy (al-awla wa al-akhlaq).

Core Idea
If knowing yourself is so hard, knowing God is infinitely harder—yet analogy lights the way.

Sanusi begins the third introduction by recalling the paradox of self-knowledge: it is the most apparent yet most difficult knowledge. He then applies this to knowledge of God, whose essence is completely unlike creation. This leads to the conclusion that human intellect cannot fully grasp God; instead, we must attribute to Him the most excellent qualities by way of analogy.

Guided Path
1
Recall the paradox
Read the first part where Sanusi restates that self-knowledge is both the clearest and most difficult of known things.
Notice how he emphasizes the extreme difficulty despite its apparent clarity.
2
Apply to God
Note that God's essence is different in quiddity from all contingent things, so knowledge of Him is even more difficult.
The contrast is key: what is clearest is still hard; what is unlike creation is harder.
3
Identify the conclusion
The intellect cannot fully encompass God; the only way is 'al-awla wa al-akhlaq' (the more appropriate and worthier).
This method means we affirm for God the most perfect attributes by analogy from creation.
4
Reflect on the method
Understand that this principle is used throughout Islamic theology to speak about God without anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : إنا قد بينا أن أظهر المعلومات هو علم كل أحد بذاته المخصوصة ونفسه (المعينة) وبينا أنه مع كونها أظهر المعلومات، فقد بلغ العلم بها إلى الغاية القصوى في الصعوبة والخفاء والغموض. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن ذات الحق سبحانه مخالفة بالماهية والحقيقة لجميع أقسام الممكنات والمحدثات فإذا كان العلم بأظهر المعلومات قد بلغ في الخفاء والغموض إلى الحد الذي ذكرناه، فالعلم الذي بصفات الموجود الذي لا يشابه شيئا (من الممكنات، ولا يناسب شيئاً) مع أنه في غاية البعد عن مناسبة المعقولات ومشابهة ما يصل إليه الفكر والذكر والوهم والخيال، لو كان صعبا عسرا كان ذلك أولى فيثبت أن هذا العلم الشريف أعلى وأجل من أن يحيط به العقل إحاطة تامة فلا سبيل للعقول البشرية فيه إلا الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق والأكمل والأفضل. واعلم أن لتقرير هذه الحجة شرحا آخر وهو: أن الاستقراء يدل على أن أظهر المعلومات عند الخلق أشياء معدودة مثل علم كل أحد بنفسه، ومثل علمه بزمانه ومكانه ومثل علمه بجسميته. ثم إن العقل إذا خاص في معرفة النفس والجسم ومعرفة المكان والزمان تحير ولم يقدر على الخلاص، فإذا كان حاله في معرفة أظهر الأشياء كذلك، فكيف يكون حاله في معرفة أخفى الأشياء. ولنبين صحة ما ذكرناه فنقول :أولها : ذاته المخصوصة وقد كشفنا حقيقة الحال فيه وثانيها علمه بالمكان والزمان فإن كل أحد يحكم ببديهة عقله أنه كان في ذلك المكان وانتقل منه إلى مكان آخر، وبقي في ذلك المكان الأول، واالعلم بالمكان جزء من أجزاء ذلك العلم. وأيضا كل أحد يحكم ببديهة عقله أن هذا الوقت الخاص وقت كذا، ثم بعده يقول : إنه مضى ذلك الوقت، وحضر وقت آخر والعلم بحقيقة الوقت والمدة جزء من العلم بأنه مضى الوقت الأول، وحضر الوقت الثاني. ثم إن العقلاء دارت رؤوسهم وحارت عقولهم في معرفة حقيقة المكان والزمان. أما المكان فأصحاب أفلاطون وكل من كان قبله من الحكماء المعتبرين: اتفقوا على أنه عبارة عن البعد الممتد . وأما اصحاب ارسطاطاليس : فقد اتفقوا على أنه عبارة عن السطح المحيط، وأن القول
The Third Introduction: We have shown that the most apparent of known things is every person's knowledge of his specific essence and his particular self, and we have shown that while it is the most apparent of known things, knowledge of it has reached the furthest extreme in difficulty, hiddenness, and obscurity. When this is established, we say: The essence of the Truth, exalted is He, is different in quiddity and reality from all categories of contingent and originated things. So if knowledge of the most apparent of known things has reached in hiddenness and obscurity to the limit we mentioned, then knowledge of the attributes of the Existent who resembles nothing (of contingent things, nor is suitable to anything), despite being at the furthest remove from suitability to intelligibles and resemblance to what thought, remembrance, understanding, and imagination attain, were it to be difficult and hard, that would be more appropriate. Thus it is established that this noble knowledge is higher and greater than that the intellect can encompass it completely, so there is no way for human intellects regarding it except to take the more appropriate, worthier, more complete, and more excellent. And know that for establishing this argument there is another explanation: that induction indicates that the most apparent of known things among creation are limited things, such as each person's knowledge of himself, his knowledge of his time and place, and his knowledge of his physical body. Then when the intellect delves into the knowledge of the soul and the body and the knowledge of space and time, it becomes confused and cannot escape. So if its state is such regarding the most apparent things, how will its state be regarding the most hidden things? And to clarify the correctness of what we have said, we say: The first of them: his specific essence, and we have uncovered the reality of the situation regarding it. The second of them: his knowledge of space and time, for everyone judges by the self-evidence of his intellect that he was in that place and moved from it to another place, and remained in that first place, and knowledge of place is part of that knowledge. Also everyone judges by the self-evidence of his intellect that this particular time is such-and-such a time, then after it he says: that time has passed, and another time has come, and knowledge of the reality of time and duration is part of the knowledge that the first time has passed and the second time has come. Then the intellects became perplexed and confused in knowing the reality of space and time. As for space, the followers of Plato and all the esteemed philosophers before him agreed that it is the extended dimension. As for the followers of Aristotle, they agreed that it is the surrounding surface, and that the statement...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a combined argument: the third introduction applies the paradox of self-knowledge to knowledge of God, and then provides an alternative inductive proof. The author first restates that self-knowledge is both the clearest and the most difficult. Then he applies this to knowledge of God: since God's essence is completely different from all contingent things, and since knowledge of the clearest thing (the self) is so difficult, knowledge of God, who is even more removed from human comprehension, must be even more difficult. Human intellects cannot fully encompass it; they can only attain knowledge by way of analogy (al-awla wa al-akhlaq): attributing to God the most appropriate, worthy, complete, and excellent attributes. The author then presents an alternative inductive proof: induction shows that the things clearest to people (self, time, place, body) become extremely difficult when examined deeply. Thus, knowledge of God, who is the most hidden, will be even more difficult. He begins to illustrate this by discussing self, then time and place. The discussion of space and time references the differing views of Platonists and Aristotelians, but the passage cuts off mid-sentence at the end of the page.

Key Terms
ذات الحق
الأولى والأخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say is the only way for human intellects to know God?
A) Direct intuition
B) Complete rational demonstration
C) The method of al-awla wa al-akhlaq
D) Sense perception

Key Takeaway
Human intellect cannot fully comprehend God, but can know Him by ascribing the most excellent attributes through the method of al-awla wa al-akhlaq.

The Inductive Proof: From Apparent to Hidden
Sanusi presents an inductive argument using self, time, and place to show that even apparent things are difficult, implying God is even more hidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inductive proof that even the most apparent things (self, time, place) become perplexing upon deep examination, demonstrating that knowledge of God (the most hidden) is even more difficult.

Core Idea
If the clearest things confuse the mind, the most hidden (God) remains beyond full grasp.

Sanusi provides another explanation for the difficulty of knowing God: induction shows that the things we consider most apparent—our own self, time, and place—become extremely confusing when examined deeply. If our intellects cannot fully grasp these, how can they grasp the most hidden reality, God? The author begins to illustrate this with examples of self, time, and place, referencing philosophical debates.

Guided Path
1
Understand the inductive claim
Read the opening where Sanusi says induction indicates that the most apparent things become difficult upon scrutiny.
He lists self, time, place, and body as examples.
2
See the analogy
The argument is: if apparent things are hard, then hidden things (God) are even harder.
This is a fortiori reasoning (al-awla).
3
Examine the first example
Sanusi refers back to the discussion of the specific essence (self), which he has already covered.
4
Examine the second example
He discusses space and time: everyone knows they are in a place and time, yet philosophers disagree on their reality.
He mentions the views of Platonists and Aristotelians on space.
5
Recognize the climax
The passage ends with the perplexity of philosophers about space, illustrating the point that even common knowledge becomes obscure.
The passage is cut off, but the argument is complete in principle.

The author first restates that self-knowledge is both the clearest and the most difficult. Then he applies this to knowledge of God: since God's essence is completely different from all contingent things, and since knowledge of the clearest thing (the self) is so difficult, knowledge of God, who is even more removed from human comprehension, must be even more difficult. Human intellects cannot fully encompass it; they can only attain knowledge by way of analogy (al-awla wa al-akhlaq): attributing to God the most appropriate, worthy, complete, and excellent attributes. The author then presents an alternative inductive proof: induction shows that the things clearest to people (self, time, place, body) become extremely difficult when examined deeply. Thus, knowledge of God, who is the most hidden, will be even more difficult. He begins to illustrate this by discussing self, then time and place. The discussion of space and time references the differing views of Platonists and Aristotelians, but the passage cuts off mid-sentence at the end of the page.

Correction: Sanusi shows that deep inquiry into space and time leads to confusion and disagreement among philosophers, illustrating that apparent things are not easily understood.

Critique of a Flawed Proof
The author criticizes a flawed proof for God's existence by highlighting its missing premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the simple dichotomy argument alone (that every existent is either necessary or contingent) is insufficient to prove the existence of a Necessary Being without first establishing the contingency of observed things.

Core Idea
Simply stating that existents are either necessary or contingent does not prove that any particular existent requires a cause.

In this passage, the author critically examines a common line of reasoning that attempts to prove the existence of a Necessary Being by merely classifying all existents as either necessary or contingent. He points out a crucial gap: without first demonstrating that the physical bodies we observe are indeed contingent, we cannot legitimately conclude that they need a preponderator. This lesson explores that critique and why it matters for sound theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's opening claim
Read the first sentence where the author states that this speech is not strong. Understand that he is critiquing a specific argument.
The author is engaging in methodological critique.
2
Understand the flawed inference
See how the argument infers from the possibility of the contingent to the necessity of a preponderator, but note that it does not establish that observed objects are contingent.
The inference is valid in the abstract, but the premises are not applied to actual existents.
3
Identify the remaining doubt
Even if the argument proved a Necessary Being, it does not identify which being is necessary—could it be bodies themselves?
This shows the argument's incompleteness.
4
Recognize the key insight
The author emphasizes that without proof that bodies are contingent, we cannot judge their need for a cause.
This is the core critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن البحث المستقصى يدل على أن هذا الكلام ليس بقوي، وذلك أنا إذا قلنا: الموجود إما واجب لذاته، أو ممكن لذاته، فإن كان ممكنا لذاته، امتنع رجحان وجوده على عدمه إلا بالمرجح، فهذا استدلال بوجود الممكن على وجود الواجب، فثبت أنه لا سبيل إلى إثبات واجب الوجود إلا بهذه المقدمة. وأيضا فهب أن الطريق الذي ذكره يدل على إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته، إلا أنه يبقى الشك في أن ذلك الموجود الواجب لذاته، هل هو هذه الأجسام أو غيرها؟ فما لم يقم البرهان على أن هذه الأجسام ممكنة لذواتها، لم يقدر على الحكم باحتياجها في وجودها إلى المؤثر والمرجح. فيثبت بما ذكرنا أن معرفة واجب الوجود لذاته، لا تحصل إلا إذا اعتبرنا أحوال وجود هذه المحسوسات، فإنا إذا بينا أنها ممكنة لذواتها، ثم بينا أن الممكن لذاته لا بد له من المرجح، ثم بينا أن التسلسل والدور باطلان، فعند ذلك يمكننا الجزم بإثبات موجود واجب الوجود لذاته. فهذا حاصل الكلام في الاستدلال بوجود غير الله على وجود الله تعالى.
Know that thorough research indicates that this speech is not strong. That is because when we say: the existent is either necessary in itself or contingent in itself, and if it is contingent in itself, its existence cannot preponderate over its nonexistence except by a preponderator, then this is an inference from the existence of the contingent to the existence of the Necessary. So it is established that there is no way to prove the Necessary Being except by this premise. Also, suppose the path he mentioned proves the Necessary Being in itself, but still doubt remains whether that existent necessary in itself is these bodies or other than them. As long as the demonstration has not been established that these bodies are contingent in themselves, one cannot judge their need for an effecter and preponderator. So it is established from what we have mentioned that knowledge of the Necessary Being in itself is only obtained if we consider the states of existence of these sensible things. For if we show that they are contingent in themselves, then show that the contingent in itself must have a preponderator, then show that infinite regress and circularity are false, then we can affirm a being that is necessary in itself. This is the conclusion of the discourse on inferring from the existence of other than God to the existence of God, exalted.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a methodological critique and correction of a common proof for the existence of the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud). The author begins by stating that careful investigation shows that a certain argument (referred to as 'this speech') is not strong. The flaw is that simply stating that the existent is either necessary or contingent, and that the contingent requires a preponderator, is insufficient. This only infers from contingent existence to a Necessary Being, but it does not establish that the sensible bodies we observe are contingent. Without proving their contingency, we cannot legitimately claim they need an external cause. The author then presents the correct method: we must start by examining sensible objects themselves. First, we demonstrate that they are contingent in themselves. Second, we establish that every contingent requires a preponderator to bring it into existence. Third, we prove that infinite regress (tasalsul) and circular causation (dawr) are impossible. With these steps, we can conclusively affirm a being that is necessary in itself. The passage thus emphasizes the importance of grounding the proof in observable reality and avoiding premature conclusions. The key terms are wajib al-wujud (necessary being), mumkin (contingent), murajjih (preponderator), tasalsul (infinite regress), and dawr (circularity).

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
مرجح
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simply declaring that all existents are either necessary or contingent is enough to prove God.
Correction: One must first prove that the observed world is contingent before using it as evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the simple dichotomy argument insufficient?
A) It does not establish that sensible objects are contingent.
B) It assumes infinite regress is impossible.
C) It relies on circular reasoning.
D) It does not use the correct terminology.

Key Takeaway
The first step in proving the Necessary Being is to demonstrate the contingency of the observable world.

The Correct Contingency Argument
The author outlines the correct three-step proof for the existence of a Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct three-step method to prove the existence of a Necessary Being: establishing contingency, the need for a preponderator, and the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity.

Core Idea
A sound proof for God requires demonstrating that objects are contingent, that contingency needs a cause, and that causal chains cannot regress infinitely.

Having critiqued the flawed argument, the author now presents the correct methodology. He emphasizes that knowledge of the Necessary Being is only attained by carefully considering the states of sensible objects. The proof proceeds in three essential steps: first, establish that these objects are contingent (mumkin); second, that every contingent requires a preponderator (murajjih); and third, that infinite regress (tasalsul) and circular causation (dawr) are impossible. Only then can we affirm a being that is necessary in itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's summary
Read the passage that begins with 'So it is established' and note the overall claim that knowledge of the Necessary Being comes from considering sensible things.
This is the conclusion of the critique and the positive presentation.
2
Identify the three steps
Look for the three conditional clauses: 'if we show that they are contingent... then show that the contingent needs a preponderator... then show that infinite regress and circularity are false'.
These are the three essential premises.
3
Understand the logical flow
See how each step builds on the previous. First establish contingency, then the need for a cause, then reject infinite regress and circularity to avoid an endless chain.
This ensures the proof concludes at a necessary being.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that with these steps we can affirm a being necessary in itself. This is the correct inference from creation to Creator.

The Dilemma of Knowing God's Essence
Author presents a dilemma regarding knowledge of God's Essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the human intellect cannot fully comprehend God's Essence, and the two problematic approaches that lead to either contingency or bewilderment.

Core Idea
God's Essence cannot be known by the intellect because similarity implies contingency and difference leads to bewilderment.

This lesson confronts a profound theological problem: how can we know God's Essence if every attempt to grasp it either reduces Him to creation or leaves the intellect helpless? The author presents a dilemma that has shaped Islamic theology. As you read the passage, consider the implications for our ability to describe God at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two opposing judgments the author presents: similarity and dissimilarity.
2
Trace the logical consequences
Follow what happens if we judge God similar to created things (contingency) or completely different (bewilderment). The author shows both paths lead to impossibility.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the author's final point: if we cannot know or conceive God's Essence, how can we describe Him? This is the 'awe-inspiring station'.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما معرفة الذات فهي أنا لو حكمنا يكون ذاته مساوية لشيء من هذه الأشياء التي أدركناها بحواسنا ووجدانات نفوسنا ووجدانات عقولنا، لزم كونه ممكنا لذاته، وهو محال وإن حكمنا يكون تلك الذات المخصوصة مخالفة لجميع هذه الموجودات التي عقلناها وعرفناها بقي العقل متحيرا والها، لا يهتدي الى شيء. فالحاصل أن العقل إذا أثبت تلك الحقيقة على وجه يصل إدراكه إليه لم يعرفه البتة، لأن كل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن الوجود لا واجب الوجود، وإن أثبته بحيث يكون مخالفا لحكمة هذه الحقائق، فحينئذ يعجز عن معرفته من حيث هو هو، وإذا عجز عن معرفته، وعن تصوره فكيف يمكنه أن يصفه بصفات الجلال والعظمة والتقديس ؟ فهذا موقف مهيب في معرفة الذات.
As for knowledge of the Essence: it is that if we judge that His Essence is equal to something of these things that we perceive by our senses, the intuitions of our souls, and the intuitions of our intellects, then it would necessitate that He be possible in Himself, which is impossible. And if we judge that that specific Essence is contrary to all these existents that we have reasoned and known, then the intellect remains bewildered, perplexed, not guided to anything. The upshot is that if the intellect affirms that reality in a way that its apprehension reaches it, it does not know it at all, because everything that is like that is possible of existence, not necessary of existence. And if it affirms it such that it is contrary to the category of these realities, then it is incapable of knowing it as it is in itself. And if it is incapable of knowing it and conceiving it, how can it describe it with attributes of majesty, greatness, and sanctification? So this is an awe-inspiring station in knowledge of the Essence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a problem statement (dilemma) regarding knowledge of God's Essence. The author presents two options: (1) If we judge God's Essence to be similar to created things we perceive, then He would be contingent (possible), which is impossible for the Necessary Being. (2) If we judge it to be completely different and beyond all known things, then the intellect is left bewildered and cannot grasp it. The author concludes that if the intellect affirms God in a way that is accessible, it fails to know Him truly (since only contingent beings can be fully known). If it affirms Him as utterly transcendent beyond all categories, it cannot know Him at all. Consequently, describing Him with positive attributes becomes problematic. This is described as an 'awe-inspiring station' (موقف مهيب).

Key Terms
معرفة الذات
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
موقف مهيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since God is known through creation, His essence can be similarly known.
Correction: The author shows the impossibility of such knowledge: either it reduces God to contingency or leaves the intellect bewildered.

Checkpoint

A) It would be possible to know Him fully
B) It would lead to the conclusion that He is contingent
C) It would be the correct way to understand His attributes
D) It would still require further investigation

Key Takeaway
The human intellect cannot know God's Essence in itself; any attempt either reduces God to contingency or leaves the intellect bewildered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading this dilemma, do you find yourself tempted to picture God in your mind despite knowing it is impossible? What will you do the next time such a thought arises to remind yourself of this awe-inspiring station?
?

When you recite Allah's names, do you ever feel a tension between the words you say and your inability to truly grasp what they mean about Him? How will you let this tension deepen your humility rather than frustrate you?

The First Five Sections of Ilahiyyat
The author enumerates the first five of ten sections of Ilahiyyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first five sections of the Science of Divinity (Ilahiyyat) as outlined by the author, including their topics and sub-sections.

Core Idea
The Science of Divinity is systematically divided into ten sections; the first five cover God's existence, negative attributes, positive attributes, actions, and creation.

The author has just established the nature of the science of divinity. Now, he presents its organizational structure by listing its ten sections. In this lesson, we will explore the first five sections, which lay the foundation for studying God's existence, attributes, acts, and creation. This will give you a clear roadmap for the study of ilahiyyat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the full translation to grasp the author's enumeration of sections.
Note the footnote markers; they indicate variant readings but don't affect the main meaning.
2
Identify the five sections
Locate each of the first five sections: existence, negative attributes, positive attributes, acts, and creation. Notice how each section is introduced.
3
Examine the sub-topics within each section
For each section, list the specific topics mentioned. For example, section two includes transcendence from multiplicity, opposition, and corporeality. Section four has three parts.
This shows the depth of the planned discussion.
4
Reflect on the structure
Consider why the author chose this order and how the sections build on each other. The first section establishes God's existence, then attributes, then acts, then creation.
This helps you internalize the logical flow of the science.
5
Test your understanding
Use the provided checkpoint and activity to verify that you can recall and apply the information from this passage.
The quiz focuses on the content of the fourth section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند هذا، يظهر على سبيل الاستقراء : أن هذا العلم يشتمل على عشرة أقسام : الأول: تقرير الدلائل (الدالة) (۱) على وجود موجود واجب الموجود لذاته . والقسم الثاني : الكلام في صفاته السلبية وهي صفات الجلال، ويدخل فيه أنه فرد مطلق (۲))، مبراً (۳) عن جميع جهات (1) الكثرة (وصفاتـه (٤) السلبية) (٥) ، ويلزمه كونه منزها عن الضد والند ويلزمه كونه منزها عن الجسمية والحيز والمكان . والقسم الثالث: الكلام في صفات الإكرام وهي العلم والقدرة والإرادة والرحمة . والقسم الرابع : الكلام في أفعاله وهو على ثلاثة أقسام. أحدها: أنه هل هو دائم في كونه فاعلا وجوادا؟ الثاني: في الكلام في كيفية صدور الأفعال عنه، ويدخل في هذا الباب . ضبط مذاهب أهل العالم، وإيراد ما في كل مذهب من تلك المذاهب من الدلائل القاصرة، ومن الشبهات الطاعنة. الثالث: الكلام في القضاء والقدر . والقسم الخامس : الكلام في شرح كلمته في تخليق العالم الأعلى والأسفل
And at this point, it appears by way of induction that this science comprises ten sections: The first: establishing the proofs (الدالة) (1) of the existence of a Being that is Necessary Being in itself. The second section: discussion of His negative attributes (الصفات السلبية), which are the attributes of majesty (صفات الجلال), and it includes that He is absolute individual (فرد مطلق) (2), transcendent (مبرأ) (3) from all aspects of multiplicity (and His negative attributes) (5), and it follows that He is transcendent (منزه) from opposition and equal, and it follows that He is transcendent from corporeality (جسمية), spatial location (حيز), and place (مكان). The third section: discussion of the attributes of honor (صفات الإكرام), which are knowledge (العلم), power (القدرة), will (الإرادة), and mercy (الرحمة). The fourth section: discussion of His acts (أفعاله), and it is divided into three parts. One: whether He is perpetual in being an agent and generous. The second: discussion of how actions emanate from Him, and this chapter includes: recording the schools of the people of the world, and presenting what is in each of those schools of defective proofs and damaging ambiguities. The third: discussion of decree and destiny (القضاء والقدر). The fifth section: discussion of explaining His word (كلمته) regarding the creation (تخليق) of the higher world (العالم الأعلى) and the lower world (العالم الأسفل).
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration. The author states that by induction, this science (العلم الإلهي) consists of ten sections, and then lists the first five. Section one deals with establishing proofs for the existence of a Necessary Being. Section two covers negative attributes (attributes of majesty), including that God is an absolute individual, transcendent from multiplicity, opposition, equal, corporeality, location, and place. Section three covers positive attributes of honor: knowledge, power, will, and mercy. Section four discusses God's acts in three parts: whether He is perpetually acting, how actions emanate from Him (including a survey of various schools and their arguments and ambiguities), and the issue of predestination (قضاء وقدر). Section five discusses the explanation of His word (كلمة) concerning the creation of the higher and lower worlds. The enumeration is presented as a clear organizational structure for the study of divinity.

Key Terms
تقرير الدلائل (establishing proofs)
صفات سلبية (negative attributes)
صفات الإكرام (attributes of honor)
الفعل (act)
قضاء وقدر (decree and destiny)
تخليق (creation)
العالم الأعلى (higher world)
العالم الأسفل (lower world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'صفات الإكرام' (attributes of honor) is a complete list of positive attributes, but later sections may add more. Also, 'كلمة' here might be misunderstood as the divine word (logos) rather than God's creative command.
Correction: The list in section three is not exhaustive; other positive attributes are discussed elsewhere. In this context, 'كلمة' refers to God's creative word or command, not the eternal speech.

The subject of Part One: proofs of God's existence
Author defines the subject of Part One.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main aims of Part One of al-Matalib al-Aliyah: proving the existence of a deity for the sensible world and establishing that the Necessary Existence is by His essence.

Core Idea
Part One of this work is a proof for God's existence and necessary being.

Before diving into philosophical arguments, the author clearly states the goal. Part One is not a general introduction but a focused investigation. It aims to prove both that there is a deity for this world and that this deity is the Necessary Existence by essence. This dual focus sets the stage for the proofs that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic heading and its literal translation once to grasp the scope of Part One.
Notice the two clauses separated by 'wa' (and).
2

Identify the two things that Part One aims to prove: (1) existence of a deity for the sensible world, (2) that the Necessary Existence is by His essence.
These are the two pillars of the section.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'al-ilah' (the deity) and 'wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi' (Necessary Existence by essence). Why is it important to specify 'by essence'?
This distinguishes God from contingent beings.
4
Reflect on the significance of 'proofs'
The author uses 'dala'il' (proofs). This indicates that Part One will present rational arguments, not mere statements.
The entire section is built on evidence.

The Six Ways to Prove the Necessary Being
Razi enumerates six methods of proving a necessary existent beyond senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six logical paths Razi identifies for proving the existence of a necessary being beyond sense perception.

Core Idea
All proofs for God reduce to six combinations of possibility and temporality.

Razi begins by noting that proving a being beyond sensory or intuitive knowledge requires rational inference: everything we perceive is in need of a cause. This need arises from possibility, temporal origination, or both—and applies either to essences or attributes. From these factors, he derives exactly six valid ways to argue for the necessary existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall structure. Notice that Razi first states the single rational path, then breaks it into three sources of need and two categories.
The passage is a preface, so it sets the stage for the whole chapter.
2
Identify the three sources of need
Find where Razi lists the three sources of need: possibility (imkān), temporal origination (ḥudūth), and their combination. Understand each term.
Possibility means something could exist or not; temporal origination means it began in time.
3
Understand the two categories
Notice that each source of need can apply to either essences (dhāt) or attributes (ṣifāt). This creates a 3×2 grid.
Essences are the things themselves; attributes are their properties.
4
List the six ways
Enumerate the six ways as Razi does: possibility of essences, possibility of attributes, origination of essences, origination of attributes, combination in essences, combination in attributes.
Memorize this classification—it organizes all classical kalām proofs.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask why Razi presents this classification. It shows that any valid theistic proof must fit one of these six patterns, giving a complete map of the rational terrain.
This sets the stage for detailed examination of each way in subsequent chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة بيان معا قد ضبط هذا الباب اعلم أنا إذا أردنا إثبات موجود لا تحكم بوجوده حواسنا ، ولا تحكم بوجوده أيضاً فطرة (1) نفوسنا وعقولنا ، فهذا مما لا سبيل إليه إلا بطريق واحد ، وهو أن يحكم عقلنا الصريح بأن هذه الموجودات التي نحكم بوجودها بحسب حواسنا ، وعقولنا ، محتاجة إما في وجودها ، أو في وجود صفة من صفاتها إلى وجود موجود غائب عن حواسنا وأوهامنا ، وبهذا الطريق يتمكن العقل من إثبات ذلك الموجود الغائب . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : منشأ الحاجة إما الإمكان ، وأما الحدوث، وإما مجموعهما ، فهذه أحوال ثلاثة وهي إما أن تعتبر في الذوات أو في الصفات ، فللمجموع طرق ستة : أولها : إمكان الذوات • وثانيها : إمكان الصفات وثالثها: حدوث الذوات . ورابعها : حدوث الصفات • وخامها : مجموع الإمكان والحدوث في الذوات وسادسها : مجموع الإمكان والحدوث في الصفات · فهذه هي الطرق التي يمكن الاستدلال بها على إثبات موجود واجب الوجود لذاته وهذا تمام الكلام في هذه المقدمة . (۱) فطرتنا وعقولنا ( س ) .
The Preface: Explanation of what has been defined in this chapter. Know that when we intend to prove an existent whose existence our senses do not judge, nor does the innate disposition (fitrah) of our souls and intellects judge its existence, then there is no way to it except by one path: that our sound intellect judges that these existents whose existence we judge by our senses and intellects are in need—either in their existence or in the existence of an attribute of their attributes—of the existence of an existent absent from our senses and imaginations. By this path the intellect can prove that absent existent. If you know this, we say: The source of need is either possibility (imkān), or temporal origination (ḥudūth), or both of them together. These are three states, and they are either considered in the essences (dhāt) or in the attributes (ṣifāt). So the total has six ways: First: possibility of essences; Second: possibility of attributes; Third: temporal origination of essences; Fourth: temporal origination of attributes; Fifth: combination of possibility and temporal origination in essences; Sixth: combination of possibility and temporal origination in attributes. These are the ways by which one can infer the existence of a being necessarily existent in itself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī). And this is the complete statement in this preface.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a preface that defines the scope of the chapter. The author states that to prove a being beyond sense perception and innate intuition, the only way is through rational inference: our senses and intellects judge the existence of certain things, and these things are in need of a cause for their existence or attributes. The need originates from possibility (imkān) or temporal origination (ḥudūth) or both. These three states can apply to either essences or attributes, yielding six total ways of inference. The author then lists them explicitly. This is a systematic enumeration of the possible arguments for the necessary existent.

Key Terms

حدوث
ذوات
صفات
موجود واجب الوجود لذاته

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Razi's six ways provide a complete map of rational proofs for the necessary being, combining possibility and temporality with essence and attributes.

Razi identifies need (ḥājah) as the root of all proofs. ?
The six ways are an exhaustive map of rational proofs, yet many Muslims never study them. Does your reliance on this knowledge—or lack of it—reveal that you have made God's existence a matter of habit rather than conviction?
